B005LRWRZ8|3.0|I think the album is just OK. There are not any songs that I dislike but there are no songs that blow me away either.   There are 2 well defined Megadeths I think - the more commercial, easy to listen, radio friendly Megadeth (born with Cowntdown...) and the more relentless, aggressive, more technical Megadeth (being Rust In Peace its maximum expression); I like this Megadeth a lot more. I've been a Megadeth fan for over 20 years, a couple of albums before Countdown, so when Countdown came out it was disappointing to me.  In Endgame, for instance, my favorite tracks are This Day We Fight and Headcrusher, followed by 1,320 and Endgame. There are other songs that I like a lot like 44 Minutes or How The Story Ends, but I enjoy these songs as a way to cool down from the heavier songs and to get ready for the next merciless attack. Th1rt3en is made solely of these radio friendly songs and so there's no excitement for me. There are songs that I was able to remember completely after the first couple of listens (Public Enemy # 1, Wrecker) and that in itself is a bad sign for me, means that the song is too easy. Easy comes, easy goes.  Most of the songs follow the easiest and most predictable song structure of intro, 2 verses, sometimes a bridge, chorus, repeat, solo, chorus, end. Fast Lane does not by incorporating a total change towards the end for the solos, but this part is way too similar to Wake Up Dead, so I wasn't all that happy with it. Maybe this is one of the most particular characteristics in this album - it's even, no ups and downs like in Endgame or United Abominations; it's even all across like Rust in Peace or Countdown. But if I could choose, I prefer an album with killer songs (Headcrusher in Endgame, Washington Is Next in United Abominations) even if there are some crappy ones (The Hardest Part in Endgame, A Tout Le Monde in UA).  Lyrical content - I usually don't pay attention to the lyrical value of the songs, but I enjoy excellent lyrics as a bonus feature and I find bad lyrics a big turn-off. Whose Life... is the perfect example - the song could be better, but the lyrics are so dumb that bring the song down. Fast Lane is yet another song about driving fast (like 1,320, 502, etc); no need for that. I like the lyrics to New World Order and Millennium (though not new) and to We The People; still miles away from Washington Is Next, United Abominations and Gears of War.  If I have to choose the highs in this album (again, not easy since it's pretty much all the same style) I'd choose New World Order (but it's not a new song, I already had it and knew it), Public Enemy (nice galloping riff), Whose Life (if it had different lyrics), Sudden Death (if it wasn't for the almost cheesy chorus) and Deadly Nightshade. The lows (not by much) are 13 and Millennium Of The Blind.   I was skeptical about this album before listening to it. The fact that they didn't work with Andy Sneap, who had produced the 2 previous albums that I like so much, and went with Johnny K, a producer that had worked solely with bands that I dislike (Disturbed, Plain White T's, Machine Head, and others) made me raise an eyebrow. Then I learned that they had only 2 months to put the album together and this reminded me that it was the last album in Megadeth's contract with RoadRunner, a label that Mustaine had criticized heavily during the release of Endgame, so it seemed that they released the album because they were forced to. And to top it all, 5 of the 13 songs were rehashed, some were as old as 17 years old. So by the time the CD got to me I wasn't all that excited. Maybe prejudice didn't let me open my mind enough? I do see that a huge majority loved this album. I just hope that Mustaine doesn't pay much attention to all these positive reviews and goes back to some more intricate song writing. B004ZY0VHY|3.0|I came across a mention of the upcoming theatrical release based on this novel, and decided after seeing the trailer that I wanted to read the book. The novel is written in one voice using a specific format: a wife (Eva) writing letters to her husband about things past. Much has already been said about the pitfalls of the author's choice to use this approach; I won't be able to add anything that hasn't already been observed by more capable reviewers. But the narration of the novel was problematic for me, in that Eva took a very long time to grow on me. Her voice and personality were well-drawn by the author; the problem was that I intensely disliked the woman...and the whole of the novel is told in her words alone. Those words are often unnecessarily frilly and show-offish, and as a reader I wondered if I should dislike Eva or the author herself for Eva's fanciful verboseness and ceaseless, insipid navel-gazing. When Eva reveals that she would've preferred a decrepit Victorian home over the modern ranch her husband chooses for them, it seemed ruefully appropriate that her character would value ostentatious architectural adornment in the form of dumbwaiters and such. Every two-dollar word chosen by the author when a more accessible and equally descriptive one would suffice had the unfortunate effect on me of delayed appreciation for my interest in the story itself. (The Kindle's built-in dictionary was made for a novel like this! When, late in the book, the author uses the word \\"pellucidity\\" I had to smile because, back in 1987, my junior year English literature teacher had a good-natured laugh over my choice to include the word \\"pellucid\\" in an essay. It was pretentious then, and it's pretentious now. It was also telling that the Kindle's dictionary had no suggestions whatsoever for the meaning of the word. So be warned: this novel is chockablock with arcane vocabulary.) However, I do feel that Eva being such an unlikeable character added dimension to the who's-at-fault-here questions the readers are likely to ask themselves.  The novel does have fine aspects, and the plot hit its stride for me somewhere in the middle. Eva may be sneeringly intelligent, but she *is* intelligent and through her the author makes some thoughtful observations about parenthood, grief and exclusion, among other things. I think most readers will guess the 'surprise' ending shortly after the halfway point, but knowing it did not diminish my enjoyment of the story as it unfolded. The author uses the timeline of the late 1990s through 2001 to address larger themes that include real-life crimes and politics of the moment, and in a few cases it added something of value to the story. Still, I've read too many novels in the recent past wherein the author's own politics come through loud and clear. I don't know if this is egregiously obvious in \\"We Need To Talk About Kevin\\", but I'd be willing to wager a hefty sum on  Ms. Shriver's general political leanings, nonetheless. I felt this takes the reader outside the immediate story, which is never a good thing. Another small point of regret for me is when Eva references a real-life school shooting and informs the reader that she has committed the names of the victims - who are too often forgotten - to memory. Unfortunately, Ms. Shriver has misspelled the name of one of these real-life victims.  Other reviewers have commented that the book is perhaps better categorized as a good ol' horror story, and to do so benefits the book enormously by allowing the reader to suspend substantial amounts of disbelief along the way. Ms. Shriver has chosen to write a very intimate tale of motherhood and children, and it shows immediately that she has no personal experience in these arenas. It is violent and graphic, and potential readers should probably have a taste for that, too.  If your vocabulary is expansive and/or you don't mind learning new words along the way and if your expectations are flexible, by all means, give \\"WeNeed To Talk About Kevin\\" a try. If you find yourself disliking Eva, I'd still recommend that you keep going. Despite its flaws, the book is a tense ride (if utterly unbelievable throughout) on a number of levels and I can honestly say it fascinated me despite its numerous, petty annoyances. In perusing the one and two-star reviews of this title, most of them make solid critiques of the book's shortcomings, and those low-rating reviews are worth checking out before you decide to part with your hard-earned money, as the author's style *and* point of view are downright polarizing.

And everyday people will buy your product or give to your organization if it helps support a significant and unique cause. Kiva.org  Kiva is a micro-loan organization that connects you (the bank) with someone who wants to start a business. So instead of them going to a loan shark, you loan $25 toget them started. Kiva has an impressive 98.96% repayment rate!  charity: water  One of my biggest reservations on donating money is not knowing exactly where my money is going. How much of what I give goes to salaries, websites and traveling expenses? Is my money even going to the cause? Blake has introduced me to charity: water, where 100% of public donations goes to 1 thing: getting people water. All other business expenses are covered by private donors. Half of the proceeds from the book Start Something That Matters goes towards just that, towards helping start organizations that matter. 1451627289|5.0|This book is one of the best I've read in awhile.  Being the first Steven King novel that I've read, I must say that this is an excellent author.  The way he describes the scenes and the characters is top notch (I'd be afraid to read his horror stories- with the ability to describe the scene this well, they must be terrifying).<br /><br />The characters in this book are believable and ones that you grow attached to.  The lead character is a complex individual who is often conflicted and alone- it makes the reader relate to him since, as we are thinking \\"what would I do if I went back in time in this situation?\\" so is he.  The lead character Jake/George (real name/alternate name he takes in the past) is the narrator, and this provides a unique glimpse into his thought processes and mindset.<br /><br />The time travel mechanism in this story is quite original, and lends itself to different scenarios than say 'Back to the Future' or other stories where the protagonist readily has a time machine at his or her disposal.  Since the nature of the time mechanism is slowly revealed over the story, and discussing it in detail would require some spoilers, it suffices to say that it is an interesting take on the time travel genre.<br /><br />While the time travel is interesting, I don't think that the main audience of this book is sci-fi junkies.  Rather, the strength lies in the characters and their relationships with one another.  The bonds that Jake/George forges with the other characters are something quite special and memorable.  When the main characters experience triumph or hardship, you feel happy or sad respectively.  They aren't simply one dimensional characters without attachment.<br /><br />Those who want to debate the book on the \\"what if\\" scenarios it creates due to changing certain events in history, I believe, have missed the mark.  King even admits in the epilogue that some of the events that unfold are only 'one of many' and not the 'only' possible outcome to the changing of certain events.<br /><br />I have read criticisms about this book based around its ending.  I will not include spoilers, but I must say that the ending was a necessary part of the story, not a disjointed separation from the rest of the story (as some have claimed).  One of the beauties of this book is that it keeps you guessing for most of the time- Up until about 90% of the way through, it is still very unclear how the story will turn out.<br /><br />Overall, this book is a masterpiece.  While King does do a decent job explaining the events surrounding the JFK assassination, I did find internet pages describing the assassination to be quite helpful in providing more details to the history of the story, as well as actual footage (readily available online) of JFK in Florida on Nov 18, 1963 (4 days before the assassination) and video of the JFK motorcade on the actual day of 11/22/63.  Such extra sources are not necessary to understand this book, but do provide greater historical perspective and thus give a greater appreciation for this book.  This book is 850+ pages, but it is one of the fastest reads I've had in a long time, especially considering the length of the book.  I couldn't put it down.  I highly recommend this book to anyone with the time to read it.the changing of certain events.<br /><br />I have read criticisms about this book based around its ending.
